<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Ludwig van Beethoven</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">German composer (1770–1827)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Beethoven" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Beethoven_(disambiguation)" title="Beethoven (disambiguation)">Beethoven (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_(disambiguation)" title="Ludwig van Beethoven (disambiguation)">Ludwig van Beethoven (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output <p><b>Ludwig van Beethoven</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[n 1]</a></sup> (baptised 17 December 1770 – 26 March 1827) was a German <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">composer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pianist" title="Pianist">pianist</a>. Beethoven remains one of the most admired composers in the history of Western music; his works rank amongst the most performed of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a> repertoire and span the <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Classical_to_Romantic_music" title="Transition from Classical to Romantic music">transition</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical period</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a> era in classical music. His career has conventionally been divided into early, middle, and late periods. His early period, during which he forged his craft, is typically considered to have lasted until 1802. From 1802 to around 1812, his middle period showed an individual development from the styles of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a>, and is sometimes characterized as heroic. During this time, he began to grow increasingly <a href="/wiki/Hearing_loss" title="Hearing loss">deaf</a>. In his late period, from 1812 to 1827, he extended his innovations in musical form and expression.
</p><p>Beethoven was born in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>. His musical talent was obvious at an early age.  He was initially harshly and intensively taught by his father <a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann van Beethoven</a>. Beethoven was later taught by the composer and conductor <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>, under whose tutelage he published his first work, a set of keyboard variations, in 1783. He found relief from a dysfunctional home life with the family of <a href="/wiki/Helene_von_Breuning" title="Helene von Breuning">Helene von Breuning</a>, whose children he loved, befriended, and taught piano. At age 21, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, which subsequently became his base, and studied composition with Haydn. Beethoven then gained a reputation as a virtuoso pianist, and he was soon patronized by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Alois,_Prince_Lichnowsky" title="Karl Alois, Prince Lichnowsky">Karl Alois, Prince Lichnowsky</a> for compositions, which resulted in his three <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trios,_Op._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trios, Op. 1 (Beethoven)">Opus 1</a> <a href="/wiki/Piano_trio" title="Piano trio">piano trios</a> (the earliest works to which he accorded an <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">opus number</a>) in 1795.
</p><p>His first major orchestral work, the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">First Symphony</a>, premiered in 1800, and his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">first set of string quartets</a> was published in 1801. Despite his hearing deteriorating during this period, he continued to conduct, premiering his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth Symphonies</a> in 1804 and 1808, respectively. His <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">Violin Concerto</a> appeared in 1806. His <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">last piano concerto</a> (No. 5, Op. 73, known as the <i>Emperor</i>), dedicated to his frequent patron <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria">Archduke Rudolf of Austria</a>, was premiered in 1811, without Beethoven as soloist. He was almost completely deaf by 1814, and he then gave up performing and appearing in public. He described his problems with health and his unfulfilled personal life in two letters, his <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt_Testament" title="Heiligenstadt Testament">Heiligenstadt Testament</a> (1802) to his brothers and his unsent love letter to an unknown "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>" (1812).
</p><p>After 1810, increasingly less socially involved, Beethoven composed many of his most admired works, including later symphonies, mature <a href="/wiki/Chamber_music" title="Chamber music">chamber music</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Late_piano_sonatas_(Beethoven)" title="Late piano sonatas (Beethoven)">late piano sonatas</a>. His only opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>, first performed in 1805, was revised to its final version in 1814. He composed <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> between 1819 and 1823 and his final Symphony, <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">No. 9</a>, one of the first examples of a <a href="/wiki/Choral_symphony" title="Choral symphony">choral symphony</a>, between 1822 and 1824. Written in his last years, his <a href="/wiki/Late_string_quartets_(Beethoven)" title="Late string quartets (Beethoven)">late string quartets</a>, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Grosse_Fuge" title="Grosse Fuge">Grosse Fuge</a></i>, of 1825–1826 are among his final achievements. After some months of bedridden illness, he <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Death of Ludwig van Beethoven">died in 1827</a>. Beethoven's works remain mainstays of the classical music repertoire.
</p>

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_early_life">Family and early life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3302" data-file-width="1996" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/170px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/255px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg/340px-Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_house_of_birth_Bonn_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's birthplace at Bonngasse 20, Bonn, now the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a> museum</div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven was the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_(1712%E2%80%931773)" title="Ludwig van Beethoven (1712–1773)">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> (1712–1773),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[n 2]</a></sup> a musician from the town of <a href="/wiki/Mechelen" title="Mechelen">Mechelen</a> in the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">Duchy of Brabant</a> (in what is now the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flemish_region" title="Flemish region">Flemish region</a> of Belgium) who had moved to <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> at the age of 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1-4">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008407_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008407-5">[3]</a></sup> Ludwig was employed as a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> singer at the court of <a href="/wiki/Clemens_August_of_Bavaria" title="Clemens August of Bavaria">Clemens August</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Cologne" title="Electorate of Cologne">Archbishop-Elector of Cologne</a>, eventually rising to become, in 1761, <a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a> (music director) and hence a pre-eminent musician in Bonn. The portrait he commissioned of himself towards the end of his life remained displayed in his grandson's rooms as a talisman of his musical heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201412–17_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201412–17-6">[4]</a></sup> Ludwig had two sons, the younger of which (<a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann</a> (1740–1792)) worked as a tenor in the same musical establishment and gave keyboard and <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> lessons to supplement his income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1-4">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Johann married <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Magdalena_Keverich" title="Maria Magdalena Keverich">Maria Magdalena Keverich</a> in 1767; she was the daughter of Heinrich Keverich (1701–1751), who had been the head chef at the court of <a href="/wiki/Johann_IX_Philipp_von_Walderdorff" title="Johann IX Philipp von Walderdorff">Johann IX Philipp von Walderdorff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Trier" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Trier">Archbishop of Trier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a50_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a50-7">[5]</a></sup> Beethoven was born of this marriage in Bonn, at what is now the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a> Museum, Bonnstrasse 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> There is no authentic record of the date of his birth; but the registry of his <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, in the Catholic Parish of St. Remigius on 17 December 1770, survives, and the custom in the region at the time was to carry out baptism within 24 hours of birth. There is a consensus (with which Beethoven himself agreed) that his birth date was 16 December, but no documentary proof of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a53_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a53-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of the seven children born to Johann van Beethoven, only Ludwig, the second-born, and two younger brothers survived infancy. <a href="/wiki/Kaspar_Anton_Karl_van_Beethoven" title="Kaspar Anton Karl van Beethoven">Kaspar Anton Karl</a> was born on 8 April 1774, and <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Nikolaus Johann van Beethoven">Nikolaus Johann</a> (generally known as Johann), the youngest, was born on 2 October 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStanley20007_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanley20007-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's first music teacher was his father. He later had other local teachers: the court <a href="/wiki/Organist" title="Organist">organist</a> Gilles van den Eeden (d. 1782), Tobias Friedrich Pfeiffer (a family friend, who provided keyboard tuition), Franz Rovantini (a relative, who instructed him in playing the violin and <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1-4">[2]</a></sup> and court concertmaster <a href="/wiki/Franz_Anton_Ries" title="Franz Anton Ries">Franz Anton Ries</a> for the violin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201474_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201474-11">[9]</a></sup> His tuition began in his fifth year. The regime was harsh and intensive, often reducing him to tears. With the involvement of the insomniac Pfeiffer, there were irregular late-night sessions, with the young Beethoven being dragged from his bed to the keyboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201422,_32_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201422,_32-12">[10]</a></sup> His musical talent was obvious at a young age. Johann, aware of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Mozart" title="Leopold Mozart">Leopold Mozart</a>'s successes in this area (with his son <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang</a> and daughter <a href="/wiki/Maria_Anna_Mozart" title="Maria Anna Mozart">Nannerl</a>), attempted to promote his son as a <a href="/wiki/Child_prodigy" title="Child prodigy">child prodigy</a>, claiming that Beethoven was six (he was seven) on the posters for his first public performance in March 1778.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a57–8_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a57–8-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1780.E2.80.931792:_Bonn"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1780–1792:_Bonn">1780–1792: Bonn</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg/150px-Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg/225px-Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg/300px-Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christian_Gottlob_Neefe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christian Gottlob Neefe. Engraving after Johann Georg Rosenberg, c. 1798</div></div></div>
<p>In 1780 or 1781, Beethoven began his studies with his most important teacher in Bonn, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199834_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199834-14">[12]</a></sup> Neefe taught him composition; in March 1783 Beethoven's first published work appeared, a set of keyboard variations (<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a> 63).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStanley20007_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanley20007-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[n 3]</a></sup> Beethoven soon began working with Neefe as assistant organist, at first unpaid (1782), and then as a paid employee (1784) of the court chapel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a65–70_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a65–70-17">[14]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Three_Piano_Sonatas,_WoO_47_(Beethoven)" title="Three Piano Sonatas, WoO 47 (Beethoven)">first three piano sonatas</a>, WoO 47, sometimes known as <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurf%C3%BCrst" title="Kurfürst">Kurfürst</a></i></span> (Elector) for their dedication to Elector <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Friedrich_von_K%C3%B6nigsegg-Rothenfels" title="Maximilian Friedrich von Königsegg-Rothenfels">Maximilian Friedrich</a> (1708–1784), were published in 1783.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a69_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a69-18">[15]</a></sup> In the same year, the first printed reference to Beethoven appeared in the <i>Magazin der Musik</i> – "Louis van Beethoven [sic] ... a boy of 11 years and most promising talent. He plays the piano very skilfully and with power, reads at sight very well ... the chief piece he plays is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Well-Tempered_Clavier" title="The Well-Tempered Clavier">Das wohltemperierte Klavier</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Sebastian Bach</a>, which Herr Neefe puts into his hands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_1-4">[2]</a></sup> Maximilian Friedrich's successor as Elector of Bonn was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Maximilian_Francis_of_Austria" title="Archduke Maximilian Francis of Austria">Maximilian Franz</a>. He gave some support to Beethoven, appointing him Court Organist and paying towards his visit to Vienna of 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a50_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a50-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199650_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199650-19">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was introduced in these years to several people who became important in his life. He often visited the cultivated von Breuning family, at whose home he taught piano to some of the children, and where the widowed Frau von Breuning offered him a motherly friendship. Here he also met <a href="/wiki/Franz_Gerhard_Wegeler" title="Franz Gerhard Wegeler">Franz Wegeler</a>, a young medical student, who became a lifelong friend (and was to marry one of the von Breuning daughters). The von Breuning family environment offered an alternative to his home life, which was increasingly dominated by his father's decline. Another frequenter of the von Breunings was <a href="/wiki/Count_Ferdinand_Ernst_Gabriel_von_Waldstein" title="Count Ferdinand Ernst Gabriel von Waldstein">Count Ferdinand von Waldstein</a>, who became a friend and financial supporter during Beethoven's Bonn period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199655_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199655-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199851–52_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199851–52-22">[19]</a></sup> Waldstein was to commission in 1791 Beethoven's first work for the stage, the ballet <i>Musik zu einem Ritterballett</i> (WoO 1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a121–122_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a121–122-23">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Count_von_Waldstein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="385" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Count_von_Waldstein.jpg/150px-Count_von_Waldstein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Count_von_Waldstein.jpg/225px-Count_von_Waldstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Count_von_Waldstein.jpg/300px-Count_von_Waldstein.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Count_von_Waldstein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Count Waldstein: portrait by <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Machek" title="Antonín Machek">Antonín Machek</a>, c. 1800</div></div></div>
<p>In the period 1785–90 there is virtually no record of Beethoven's activity as a composer. This may be attributed to the lukewarm response his initial publications had attracted, and also to ongoing problems in the Beethoven family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199836–37_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199836–37-24">[21]</a></sup> His mother died in 1787, shortly after Beethoven's first visit to Vienna, where he stayed for about two weeks and almost certainly met Mozart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2-20">[17]</a></sup> In 1789 Beethoven's father was forcibly retired from the service of the Court (as a consequence of his alcoholism) and it was ordered that half of his father's pension be paid directly to Ludwig for support of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a95_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a95-25">[22]</a></sup> He contributed further to the family's income by teaching (to which Wegeler said he had "an extraordinary aversion"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199851_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199851-26">[23]</a></sup>) and by playing <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a> in the court orchestra. This familiarized him with a variety of operas, including works by Mozart, <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Willibald_Gluck" title="Christoph Willibald Gluck">Gluck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Paisiello" title="Giovanni Paisiello">Paisiello</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a95–98_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a95–98-27">[24]</a></sup> Here he also befriended <a href="/wiki/Anton_Reicha" title="Anton Reicha">Anton Reicha</a>, a composer, <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flautist</a> and violinist of about his own age who was a nephew of the court orchestra's conductor, <a href="/wiki/Josef_Reicha" title="Josef Reicha">Josef Reicha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a96_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a96-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1790 to 1792, Beethoven composed several works (none were published at the time) showing a growing range and maturity. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musicologist" title="Musicologist">Musicologists</a> have identified a theme similar to those of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in a set of variations written in 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835–41_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835–41-29">[26]</a></sup> It was perhaps on Neefe's recommendation that Beethoven received his first commissions; the Literary Society in Bonn commissioned a cantata to mark the occasion of the death in 1790 of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Joseph II</a> (WoO 87), and a further cantata, to celebrate the subsequent accession of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Leopold II</a> as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> (WoO 88), may have been commissioned by the Elector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199693–94_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199693–94-30">[27]</a></sup> These two <i>Emperor Cantatas</i> were never performed at the time and they remained lost until the 1880s when they were described by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a> as "Beethoven through and through" and as such prophetic of the style which would mark his music as distinct from the classical tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014107–111_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014107–111-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven was probably first introduced to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> in late 1790 when the latter was travelling to London and stopped in Bonn around Christmas time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835-32">[29]</a></sup> A year and a half later, they met in Bonn on Haydn's return trip from London to Vienna in July 1792, when Beethoven played in the orchestra at the <a href="/wiki/Redoute,_Bad_Godesberg" title="Redoute, Bad Godesberg">Redoute in Godesberg</a>. Arrangements were likely made at that time for Beethoven to study with the older master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200841_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200841-33">[30]</a></sup> Waldstein wrote to him before his departure: "You are going to Vienna in fulfilment of your long-frustrated wishes ... With the help of assiduous labour you shall receive Mozart's spirit from Haydn's hands."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_2-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1792.E2.80.931802:_Vienna_.E2.80.93_the_early_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1792–1802:_Vienna_–_the_early_years">1792–1802: Vienna – the early years</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Beethoven as a young man, c. 1800, by Carl Traugott Riedel (1769–1832)</div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven left Bonn for Vienna in November 1792, amid rumours of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Coalition" title="First Coalition">war spilling out of France</a>; he learned shortly after his arrival that his father had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a34–36_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a34–36-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200842_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200842-35">[32]</a></sup> Over the next few years, Beethoven responded to the widespread feeling that he was a successor to the recently deceased Mozart by studying that master's work and writing works with a distinctly Mozartian flavour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200843_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200843-36">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>He did not immediately set out to establish himself as a composer, but rather devoted himself to study and performance. Working under Haydn's direction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_3_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_3-37">[34]</a></sup> he sought to master <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>. He also studied violin under <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Schuppanzigh" title="Ignaz Schuppanzigh">Ignaz Schuppanzigh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200847,_54_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200847,_54-38">[35]</a></sup> Early in this period, he also began receiving occasional instruction from <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Salieri" title="Antonio Salieri">Antonio Salieri</a>, primarily in Italian vocal composition style; this relationship persisted until at least 1802, and possibly as late as 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a149_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a149-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>With Haydn's departure for England in 1794, Beethoven was expected by the Elector to return home to Bonn. He chose instead to remain in Vienna, continuing his instruction in counterpoint with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Albrechtsberger" title="Johann Georg Albrechtsberger">Johann Albrechtsberger</a> and other teachers. In any case, by this time it must have seemed clear to his employer that Bonn would fall to the French, as it did in October 1794, effectively leaving Beethoven without a stipend or the necessity to return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERonge2013_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERonge2013-40">[37]</a></sup> However, several Viennese noblemen had already recognised his ability and offered him financial support, among them <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Franz_von_Lobkowitz" title="Joseph Franz von Lobkowitz">Prince Joseph Franz Lobkowitz</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Alois_Johann-Nepomuk_Vinzenz,_F%C3%BCrst_Lichnowsky" title="Karl Alois Johann-Nepomuk Vinzenz, Fürst Lichnowsky">Prince Karl Lichnowsky</a>, and Baron <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_van_Swieten" title="Gottfried van Swieten">Gottfried van Swieten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Assisted by his connections with Haydn and Waldstein, Beethoven began to develop a reputation as a performer and improviser in the salons of the Viennese nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199859_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199859-42">[39]</a></sup> His friend <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Simrock" title="Nikolaus Simrock">Nikolaus Simrock</a> began publishing his compositions, starting with a set of keyboard variations on a theme of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ditters_von_Dittersdorf" title="Carl Ditters von Dittersdorf">Dittersdorf</a> (WoO 66).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200846_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200846-43">[40]</a></sup> By 1793, he had established a reputation in Vienna as a piano virtuoso, but he apparently withheld works from publication so that their eventual appearance would have greater impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="463" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg/150px-%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg/225px-%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg/300px-%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%96lenhainz_-_Franz_Joseph_Maximilian_von_Lobkowitz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prince Lobkowitz: portrait by <a href="/wiki/August_Friedrich_Oelenhainz" title="August Friedrich Oelenhainz">August Friedrich Oelenhainz</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1795 Beethoven made his public debut in Vienna over three days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014174–75_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014174–75-44">[41]</a></sup> beginning with a performance of one of his own <a href="/wiki/Piano_concerto" title="Piano concerto">piano concertos</a> on 29 March at the <a href="/wiki/Burgtheater" title="Burgtheater">Burgtheater</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[n 4]</a></sup> and ending with a Mozart concerto on 31 March, probably the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._20_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 20 (Mozart)">D minor concerto</a> for which he had written a <a href="/wiki/Cadenza" title="Cadenza">cadenza</a> soon after his arrival in Vienna. By this year he had two piano concertos available for performance, one in <a href="/wiki/B-flat_major" title="B-flat major">B-flat major</a> he had begun composing before moving to Vienna and had worked on for over a decade, and one in <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a> composed for the most part during 1795.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014176–77_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014176–77-50">[46]</a></sup> Viewing the latter as the more substantive work, he chose to designate it as his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 1 (Beethoven)">first piano concerto</a>, publishing it in March 1801 as Opus 15, before publishing the former as <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 2 (Beethoven)">Opus 19</a> the following December. He wrote new cadenzas for both works in 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after his public debut he arranged for the publication of the first of his compositions to which he assigned an <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">opus number</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trios,_Op._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trios, Op. 1 (Beethoven)">three piano trios, Opus 1</a>. These works were dedicated to his patron Prince Lichnowsky,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-45">[42]</a></sup> and were a financial success; Beethoven's profits were nearly sufficient to cover his living expenses for a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200856_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200856-52">[48]</a></sup> In 1799 Beethoven participated in (and won) a notorious piano 'duel' at the home of Baron Raimund Wetzlar (a former patron of Mozart) against the virtuoso <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_W%C3%B6lfl" title="Joseph Wölfl">Joseph Wölfl</a>; and in the following year he similarly triumphed against <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Steibelt" title="Daniel Steibelt">Daniel Steibelt</a> at the salon of <a href="/wiki/Count_Moritz_von_Fries" title="Count Moritz von Fries">Count Moritz von Fries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199879_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199879-53">[49]</a></sup> Beethoven's eighth <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonata</a> the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 8 (Beethoven)">Pathétique</a> (Op. 13), published in 1799 is described by the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Barry_Cooper_(musicologist)" title="Barry Cooper (musicologist)">Barry Cooper</a> as "surpass[ing] any of his previous compositions, in strength of character, depth of emotion, level of originality, and ingenuity of motivic and tonal manipulation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200882_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200882-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven composed his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">first six string quartets (Op. 18)</a> between 1798 and 1800 (commissioned by, and dedicated to, Prince Lobkowitz). They were published in 1801. He also completed his <a href="/wiki/Septet_(Beethoven)" title="Septet (Beethoven)">Septet</a> (Op. 20) in 1799, which was one of his most popular works during his lifetime. With premieres of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a> Symphonies in 1800 and 1803, he became regarded as one of the most important of a generation of young composers following Haydn and Mozart. But his melodies, musical development, use of modulation and texture, and characterisation of emotion all set him apart from his influences, and heightened the impact some of his early works made when they were first published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200858_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200858-55">[51]</a></sup> For the premiere of his First Symphony, he hired the <a href="/wiki/Burgtheater" title="Burgtheater">Burgtheater</a> on 2 April 1800, and staged an extensive programme, including works by Haydn and Mozart, as well as his Septet, the Symphony, and one of his piano concertos (the latter three works all then unpublished). The concert, which the <i><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_musikalische_Zeitung" title="Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung">Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</a></i> described as "the most interesting concert in a long time", was not without difficulties; among the criticisms was that "the players did not bother to pay any attention to the soloist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200890_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200890-56">[52]</a></sup> By the end of 1800, Beethoven and his music were already much in demand from patrons and publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200897_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200897-57">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1234" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg/150px-Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg/225px-Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg/300px-Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Josephine_Brunsvik.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Josephine Brunsvik, pencil miniature (unknown artist), before 1804</div></div></div>
<p>In May 1799, he taught piano to the daughters of Hungarian Countess Anna Brunsvik. During this time, he fell in love with the younger daughter <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Brunsvik" title="Josephine Brunsvik">Josephine</a>. Amongst his other students, from 1801 to 1805, he tutored <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ries" title="Ferdinand Ries">Ferdinand Ries</a>, who went on to become a composer and later wrote about their encounters. The young <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, who later became a renowned music teacher himself, studied with Beethoven from 1801 to 1803. In late 1801, he met a young countess, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Guicciardi" title="Julie Guicciardi">Julie Guicciardi</a>, through the Brunsvik family; he mentions his love for Julie in a November 1801 letter to a friend, but class difference prevented any consideration of pursuing this. He dedicated his 1802 <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Sonata Op. 27 No. 2</a>, now commonly known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Moonlight Sonata</a></i>, to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteblin2009_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteblin2009-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1801 he completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creatures_of_Prometheus" title="The Creatures of Prometheus">The Creatures of Prometheus</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>. The work received numerous performances in 1801 and 1802, and he rushed to publish a piano arrangement to capitalise on its early popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200898–103_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200898–103-59">[55]</a></sup> In the spring of 1802 he completed the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second Symphony</a>, intended for performance at a concert that was cancelled. The symphony received its premiere instead at a subscription concert in April 1803 at the <a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, where he had been appointed composer in residence. In addition to the Second Symphony, the concert also featured the First Symphony, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Piano Concerto</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_on_the_Mount_of_Olives_(Beethoven)" title="Christ on the Mount of Olives (Beethoven)">Christ on the Mount of Olives</a></i>. Reviews were mixed, but the concert was a financial success; he was able to charge three times the cost of a typical concert ticket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–27_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–27-60">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>His business dealings with publishers also began to improve in 1802 when his brother Kaspar, who had previously assisted him casually, began to assume a larger role in the management of his affairs. In addition to negotiating higher prices for recently composed works, Kaspar also began selling some of his earlier unpublished compositions and encouraged him (against Beethoven's preference) to also make arrangements and transcriptions of his more popular works for other instrument combinations. Beethoven acceded to these requests, as he could not prevent publishers from hiring others to do similar arrangements of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–115_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112–115-61">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1802.E2.80.931812:_The_.27heroic.27_period"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1802–1812:_The_'heroic'_period">1802–1812: The 'heroic' period</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deafness">Deafness</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1803, painted by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Horneman" title="Christian Horneman">Christian Horneman</a></div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven told the English pianist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Neate" title="Charles Neate">Charles Neate</a> (in 1815) that he dated his hearing loss from a fit in 1798 induced by a quarrel with a singer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998160_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998160-62">[58]</a></sup> During its gradual decline, his hearing was further impeded by a severe form of <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">tinnitus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014223–224_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014223–224-63">[59]</a></sup> As early as 1801, he wrote to Wegeler and another friend Karl Amenda, describing his symptoms and the difficulties they caused in both professional and social settings (although it is likely some of his close friends were already aware of the problems).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008108_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008108-64">[60]</a></sup> The cause was probably <a href="/wiki/Otosclerosis" title="Otosclerosis">otosclerosis</a>, perhaps accompanied by degeneration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auditory_nerve" title="Auditory nerve">auditory nerve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[n 5]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the advice of his doctor, Beethoven moved to the small Austrian town of <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt,_Vienna" title="Heiligenstadt, Vienna">Heiligenstadt</a>, just outside Vienna, from April to October 1802 in an attempt to come to terms with his condition. There he wrote the document now known as the <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt_Testament" title="Heiligenstadt Testament">Heiligenstadt Testament</a>, a letter to his brothers which records his thoughts of suicide due to his growing deafness and records his resolution to continue living for and through his art. The letter was never sent and was discovered in his papers after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996169–172_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996169–172-69">[64]</a></sup> The letters to Wegeler and Amenda were not so despairing; in them Beethoven commented also on his ongoing professional and financial success at this period, and his determination, as he expressed it to Wegeler, to "seize Fate by the throat; it shall certainly not crush me completely".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5-65">[61]</a></sup> In 1806, Beethoven noted on one of his musical sketches: "Let your deafness no longer be a secret – even in art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998162_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998162-70">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's hearing loss did not prevent him from composing music, but it made playing at concerts—an important source of income at this phase of his life—increasingly difficult. (It also contributed substantially to his social withdrawal.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_5-65">[61]</a></sup> Czerny remarked however that Beethoven could still hear speech and music normally until 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaly1994262_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaly1994262-71">[66]</a></sup> Beethoven never became totally deaf; in his final years he was still able to distinguish low tones and sudden loud sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_heroic_style">The heroic style</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg/220px-Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg/330px-Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg/440px-Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eroica_Beethoven_title.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titlepage of ms. of the <i>Eroica</i> Symphony, with Napoleon's name scored through by Beethoven</div></div></div>
<p>Beethoven's return to Vienna from Heiligenstadt was marked by a change in musical style, and is now often designated as the start of his middle or "heroic" period, characterised by many original works composed on a grand scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyson1969138–141_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyson1969138–141-73">[68]</a></sup> According to Carl Czerny, Beethoven said: "I am not satisfied with the work I have done so far. From now on I intend to take a new way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008131_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008131-74">[69]</a></sup> An early major work employing this new style was the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in E-flat, Op. 55, known as the <i>Eroica</i>, written in 1803–04. The idea of creating a symphony based on the career of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> may have been suggested to Beethoven by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_XIV_John_of_Sweden" title="Charles XIV John of Sweden">General Bernadotte</a> in 1798.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_4_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_4-75">[70]</a></sup> Beethoven, sympathetic to the ideal of the heroic revolutionary leader, originally gave the symphony the title "Bonaparte", but disillusioned by Napoleon <a href="/wiki/1804_French_constitutional_referendum" title="1804 French constitutional referendum">declaring himself Emperor in 1804</a>, he scratched Napoleon's name from the manuscript's title page, and the symphony was published in 1806 with its present title and the subtitle "to celebrate the memory of a great man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_6_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_6-76">[71]</a></sup> The <i>Eroica</i> was longer and larger in scope than any previous symphony. When it premiered in early 1805 it received a mixed reception. Some listeners objected to its length or misunderstood its structure, while others viewed it as a masterpiece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008148_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008148-77">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E._T._A._Hoffmann,_autorretrato.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2544" data-file-width="2188" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg/170px-E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg/255px-E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg/340px-E._T._A._Hoffmann%2C_autorretrato.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E._T._A._Hoffmann,_autorretrato.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>E. T. A. Hoffmann, <a href="/wiki/Self-portrait" title="Self-portrait">self-portrait</a>, c. 1820</div></div></div>
<p>Other middle period works extend in the same dramatic manner the musical language Beethoven had inherited. The <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._7%E2%80%939,_Op._59_%E2%80%93_Rasumovsky_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59 – Rasumovsky (Beethoven)">Rasumovsky</a> string quartets, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._23_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 23 (Beethoven)">Appassionata</a></i> piano sonatas share the heroic spirit of the Third Symphony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_6_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_6-76">[71]</a></sup> Other works of this period include the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._4_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 4 (Beethoven)">Fourth</a> through <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 8 (Beethoven)">Eighth</a> Symphonies, the oratorio <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_on_the_Mount_of_Olives_(Beethoven)" title="Christ on the Mount of Olives (Beethoven)">Christ on the Mount of Olives</a></i>, the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">Violin Concerto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_14_and_15_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_14_and_15-78">[73]</a></sup> Beethoven was hailed in 1810 by the writer and composer <a href="/wiki/E._T._A._Hoffmann" title="E. T. A. Hoffmann">E. T. A. Hoffmann</a>, in an influential review in the <i><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_musikalische_Zeitung" title="Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung">Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</a></i>, as the greatest of (what he considered) the three <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a> composers (that is, ahead of Haydn and Mozart); in Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth Symphony</a> his music, wrote Hoffmann, "sets in motion terror, fear, horror, pain, and awakens the infinite yearning that is the essence of romanticism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassedy20101–6_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassedy20101–6-79">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time Beethoven's income came from publishing his works, from performances of them, and from his patrons, for whom he gave private performances and copies of works they commissioned for an exclusive period before their publication. Some of his early patrons, including Prince Lobkowitz and Prince Lichnowsky, gave him annual stipends in addition to commissioning works and purchasing published works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200878–79_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200878–79-80">[75]</a></sup> Perhaps his most important aristocratic patron was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria">Archduke Rudolf of Austria</a>, the youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Leopold II</a>, who in 1803 or 1804 began to study piano and composition with him. They became friends, and their meetings continued until 1824.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300–301_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300–301-81">[76]</a></sup> Beethoven dedicated 14 compositions to Rudolf, including some of his major works such as the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trio,_Op._97_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trio, Op. 97 (Beethoven)"><i>Archduke</i> Trio</a> Op. 97 (1811) and <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> Op. 123 (1823).
</p><p>His position at the Theater an der Wien was terminated when the theatre changed management in early 1804, and he was forced to move temporarily to the suburbs of Vienna with his friend Stephan von Breuning. This slowed work on <i>Leonore</i> (his original title for his opera), his largest work to date, for a time. It was delayed again by the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censor</a> and finally premiered, under its present title of <i>Fidelio</i>, in November 1805 to houses that were nearly empty because of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Third_Coalition" title="War of the Third Coalition">French occupation of the city</a>. In addition to being a financial failure, this version of <i>Fidelio</i> was also a critical failure, and Beethoven began revising it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008150_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008150-82">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite this failure, Beethoven continued to attract recognition. In 1807 the musician and publisher <a href="/wiki/Muzio_Clementi" title="Muzio Clementi">Muzio Clementi</a> secured the rights for publishing his works in England, and Haydn's former patron <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_II,_Prince_Esterh%C3%A1zy" title="Nikolaus II, Prince Esterházy">Prince Esterházy</a> commissioned a mass (the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_C_major_(Beethoven)" title="Mass in C major (Beethoven)">Mass in C</a>, Op. 86) for his wife's name-day. But he could not count on such recognition alone. A <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">colossal benefit concert</a> which he organized in December 1808, and was widely advertised, included the premieres of the Fifth and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Sixth (<i>Pastoral</i>)</a> symphonies, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._4_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 4 (Beethoven)">Fourth Piano Concerto</a>, extracts from the Mass in C, the <a href="/wiki/Aria" title="Aria">scena and aria</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ah!_perfido" title="Ah! perfido">Ah! perfido</a></i> Op. 65 and the <a href="/wiki/Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)" title="Choral Fantasy (Beethoven)">Choral Fantasy op. 80</a>. There was a large audience (including Czerny and the young <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Moscheles" title="Ignaz Moscheles">Ignaz Moscheles</a>), but it was under-rehearsed, involved many stops and starts, and during the Fantasia Beethoven was noted shouting at the musicians "badly played, wrong, again!" The financial outcome is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a445–448_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a445–448-83">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1829" data-file-width="1378" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/170px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/255px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/340px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's patron, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria_(1788%E2%80%931831)" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria (1788–1831)">Archduke Rudolf</a>; portrait by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptist_von_Lampi_the_Elder" title="Johann Baptist von Lampi the Elder">Johann Baptist von Lampi</a></div></div></div><p>In the autumn of 1808, after having been rejected for a position at the Royal Theatre, Beethoven had received an offer from Napoleon's brother <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte" title="Jérôme Bonaparte">Jérôme Bonaparte</a>, then king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Westphalia" title="Kingdom of Westphalia">Westphalia</a>, for a well-paid position as <a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a> at the court in <a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Cassel</a>. To persuade him to stay in Vienna, Archduke Rudolf, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand,_5th_Prince_Kinsky_of_Wchinitz_and_Tettau" title="Ferdinand, 5th Prince Kinsky of Wchinitz and Tettau">Prince Kinsky</a> and Prince Lobkowitz, after receiving representations from Beethoven's friends, pledged to pay him a pension of 4000 florins a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a457_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a457-84">[79]</a></sup> In the event, Archduke Rudolf paid his share of the pension on the agreed date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008195_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008195-85">[80]</a></sup> Kinsky, immediately called to military duty, did not contribute and died in November 1812 after falling from his horse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199648_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199648-86">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200848_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200848-87">[82]</a></sup> The Austrian currency destabilized and Lobkowitz went bankrupt in 1811 so that to benefit from the agreement Beethoven eventually had recourse to the law, which in 1815 brought him some recompense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998194_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998194-88">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">imminence of war</a> reaching Vienna itself was felt in early 1809. In April, Beethoven completed writing his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">Piano Concerto No. 5</a> in E flat major, Op. 73,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199648_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199648-86">[81]</a></sup> which the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Einstein" title="Alfred Einstein">Alfred Einstein</a> has described as "the apotheosis of the military concept" in Beethoven's music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinstein1958248_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinstein1958248-89">[84]</a></sup> Archduke Rudolf left the capital with the Imperial family in early May, prompting Beethoven's piano sonata <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._26_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 26 (Beethoven)">Les Adieux</a></i> (Sonata No. 26, Op. 81a), actually entitled by Beethoven in German <i>Das Lebewohl</i> (The Farewell), of which the final movement, <i>Das Wiedersehen</i> (The Return), is dated in the manuscript with the date of Rudolf's homecoming of 30 January 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a464_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a464-90">[85]</a></sup> During the French bombardment of Vienna in May, Beethoven took refuge in the cellar of the house of his brother Kaspar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a465_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a465-91">[86]</a></sup> The subsequent occupation of Vienna and the disruptions to cultural life and to Beethoven's publishers, together with Beethoven's poor health at the end of 1809, explain his significantly reduced output during this period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a467–473_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a467–473-92">[87]</a></sup> although other notable works of the year include his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._10_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 10 (Beethoven)">String Quartet No. 10</a> in E flat major, Op. 74 (known as <i>The Harp</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._24_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 24 (Beethoven)">Piano Sonata No. 24</a> in F sharp major op. 78, dedicated to Josephine's sister <a href="/wiki/Therese_Brunsvik" title="Therese Brunsvik">Therese Brunsvik</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a475_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a475-93">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Goethe">Goethe</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3084" data-file-width="2643" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg/150px-Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg/225px-Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg/300px-Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goethe_K%C3%BCgelgen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goethe in 1808; portrait by <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_von_K%C3%BCgelgen" title="Gerhard von Kügelgen">Gerhard von Kügelgen</a></div></div></div><p> At the end of 1809 Beethoven was commissioned to write incidental music for <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>'s play <i><a href="/wiki/Egmont_(play)" title="Egmont (play)">Egmont</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Egmont_(Beethoven)" title="Egmont (Beethoven)">The result</a> (an overture, and nine additional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entracte" title="Entracte">entractes</a> and vocal pieces, Op. 84), which appeared in 1810, fitted well with Beethoven's heroic style and he became interested in Goethe, setting three of his poems as songs (Op. 83) and learning about the poet from a mutual acquaintance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bettina_Brentano" title="Bettina Brentano">Bettina Brentano</a> (who also wrote to Goethe at this time about Beethoven). Other works of this period in a similar vein were the F minor <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._11_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 11 (Beethoven)">String Quartet Op. 95</a>, to which Beethoven gave the subtitle <i>Quartetto serioso</i>, and the Op. 97 <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trio,_Op._97_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trio, Op. 97 (Beethoven)">Piano Trio in B flat major</a> known, from its dedication to his patron Rudolph as the <i>Archduke Trio</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_7_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_7-94">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1811, Beethoven became seriously ill, having headaches and high fever. His doctor <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptist_Malfatti_von_Monteregio" title="Johann Baptist Malfatti von Monteregio">Johann Malfatti</a> recommended him to take a cure at the <a href="/wiki/Spa" title="Spa">spa</a> of <a href="/wiki/Teplice" title="Teplice">Teplitz</a> (now Teplice in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>), where he wrote two more overtures and sets of incidental music for dramas, this time by <a href="/wiki/August_von_Kotzebue" title="August von Kotzebue">August von Kotzebue</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/King_Stephen_(Beethoven)" title="King Stephen (Beethoven)">King Stephen</a></i> Op. 117 and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ruins_of_Athens" title="The Ruins of Athens">The Ruins of Athens</a></i> Op. 113. Advised again to visit Teplitz in 1812 he met there with Goethe, who wrote: "His talent amazed me; unfortunately he is an utterly untamed personality, who is not altogether wrong in holding the world to be detestable, but surely does not make it any more enjoyable ... by his attitude." Beethoven wrote to his publishers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breitkopf_and_H%C3%A4rtel" title="Breitkopf and Härtel">Breitkopf and Härtel</a> that "Goethe delights far too much in the court atmosphere, far more than is becoming in a poet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_7_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_7-94">[89]</a></sup> But following their meeting he began a setting for choir and orchestra of <a href="/wiki/Meeresstille_und_gl%C3%BCckliche_Fahrt_(Beethoven)" title="Meeresstille und glückliche Fahrt (Beethoven)">Goethe's <i>Meeresstille und glückliche Fahrt</i></a> <i>(Calm Sea and Prosperous Voyage)</i> (Op. 112), completed in 1815. After this was published in 1822 with a dedication to the poet, Beethoven wrote to him: "The admiration, the love and esteem which already in my youth I cherished for the one and only immortal Goethe have persisted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199647_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199647-95">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Immortal_Beloved">The Immortal Beloved</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antonie_Brentano3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2334" data-file-width="1506" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Antonie_Brentano3.jpg/150px-Antonie_Brentano3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Antonie_Brentano3.jpg/225px-Antonie_Brentano3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Antonie_Brentano3.jpg/300px-Antonie_Brentano3.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antonie_Brentano3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antonie Brentano (1808) painted by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Karl_Stieler" title="Joseph Karl Stieler">Joseph Karl Stieler</a></div></div></div><p>While he was at Teplitz in 1812 he wrote a ten-page love letter to his "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>", which he never sent to its addressee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582-96">[91]</a></sup> The identity of the intended recipient was long a subject of debate, although the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Maynard_Solomon" title="Maynard Solomon">Maynard Solomon</a> has convincingly demonstrated that the intended recipient must have been <a href="/wiki/Antonie_Brentano" title="Antonie Brentano">Antonie Brentano</a>; other candidates have included Julie Guicciardi, <a href="/wiki/Therese_Malfatti" title="Therese Malfatti">Therese Malfatti</a> and Josephine Brunsvik.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996107_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996107-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[n 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>All of these had been regarded by Beethoven as possible soulmates during his first decade in Vienna. Guicciardi, although she flirted with Beethoven, never had any serious interest in him and married <a href="/wiki/Wenzel_Robert_von_Gallenberg" title="Wenzel Robert von Gallenberg">Wenzel Robert von Gallenberg</a> in November 1803. (Beethoven insisted to his later secretary and biographer, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, that Gucciardi had "sought me out, crying, but I scorned her".)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998196–197_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998196–197-100">[94]</a></sup> Josephine had, since Beethoven's initial infatuation with her, married the elderly Count Joseph Deym, who died in 1804. Beethoven began to visit her and commenced a passionate correspondence. Initially, he accepted that Josephine could not love him, but he continued to address himself to her even after she had moved to Budapest, finally demonstrating that he had got the message in his last letter to her of 1807: "I thank you for wishing still to appear as if I were not altogether banished from your memory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998197–199_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998197–199-101">[95]</a></sup> Malfatti was the niece of Beethoven's doctor, and he had proposed to her in 1810. He was 40, she was 19 – the proposal was rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998196_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998196-102">[96]</a></sup> She is now remembered as the recipient of the piano <a href="/wiki/Bagatelle_(music)" title="Bagatelle (music)">bagatelle</a> <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCr_Elise" title="Für Elise">Für Elise</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199620_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199620-103">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[n 7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antonie (Toni) Brentano (née von Birkenstock), ten years younger than Beethoven, was the wife of Franz Brentano, the half-brother of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bettina_Brentano" title="Bettina Brentano">Bettina Brentano</a>, who provided Beethoven's introduction to the family. It would seem that Antonie and Beethoven had an affair during 1811–1812. Antonie left Vienna with her husband in late 1812 and never met with (or apparently corresponded with) Beethoven again, although in her later years she wrote and spoke fondly of him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998231–239_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998231–239-106">[99]</a></sup> Some speculate that Beethoven was the father of Antonie's son Karl Josef, though the two never met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1812 there are no reports of any romantic liaisons of Beethoven; it is, however, clear from his correspondence of the period and, later, from the conversation books, that he would occasionally meet with prostitutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998284,_339–340_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998284,_339–340-108">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1813.E2.80.931822:_Acclaim"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1813–1822:_Acclaim">1813–1822: Acclaim</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_problems">Family problems</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg/150px-Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg/225px-Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg/300px-Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_van_Beethoven_Miniature_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Karl van Beethoven, c. 1820: miniature portrait by unknown artist</div></div></div><p>In early 1813 Beethoven apparently went through a difficult emotional period, and his compositional output dropped. His personal appearance degraded—it had generally been neat—as did his manners in public, notably when dining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998284–285_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998284–285-109">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Family issues may have played a part in this. Beethoven had visited his brother Johann at the end of October 1812. He wished to end Johann's cohabitation with Therese Obermayer, a woman who already had an illegitimate child. He was unable to convince Johann to end the relationship and appealed to the local civic and religious authorities, but Johann and Therese married on 8 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998282_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998282-110">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The illness and eventual death of his brother Kaspar from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> became an increasing concern. Kaspar had been ill for some time; in 1813 Beethoven lent him 1500 <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_florin" title="Austro-Hungarian florin">florins</a>, to procure the repayment of which he was ultimately led to complex legal measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998301–302_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998301–302-111">[104]</a></sup> After Kaspar died on 15 November 1815, Beethoven immediately became embroiled in a protracted legal dispute with Kaspar's wife <a href="/wiki/Johanna_van_Beethoven" title="Johanna van Beethoven">Johanna</a> over custody of their son <a href="/wiki/Karl_van_Beethoven" title="Karl van Beethoven">Karl</a>, then nine years old. Beethoven had successfully applied to Kaspar to have himself named the sole guardian of the boy. A late <a href="/wiki/Codicil_(will)" title="Codicil (will)">codicil</a> to Kaspar's will gave him and Johanna joint guardianship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998302–303_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998302–303-112">[105]</a></sup> While Beethoven was successful at having his nephew removed from her custody in January 1816, and had him removed to a private school<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303-113">[106]</a></sup> in 1818 he was again preoccupied with the legal processes around Karl. While giving evidence to the court for the <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Landrecht_(medieval)" title="Landrecht (medieval)">Landrechte</a>, Beethoven was unable to prove that he was of noble birth and as a consequence, on 18 December 1818 the case was transferred to the civil <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">magistrate</a> of Vienna, where he lost sole guardianship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303-113">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[n 8]</a></sup> He only regained custody after intensive legal struggles in 1820.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998316–321_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998316–321-115">[107]</a></sup> During the years that followed, Beethoven frequently interfered in his nephew's life in what Karl perceived as an overbearing manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998364–365_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998364–365-116">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_Vienna">Post-war Vienna</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="980" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/170px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/255px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/340px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1815: portrait by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Willibrord_M%C3%A4hler" title="Joseph Willibrord Mähler">Joseph Willibrord Mähler</a></div></div></div><p>Beethoven was finally motivated to begin significant composition again in June 1813, when news arrived of Napoleon's defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vitoria" title="Battle of Vitoria">Battle of Vitoria</a> by a coalition led by the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>. The inventor Mälzel persuaded him to write a work commemorating the event for his mechanical instrument the <a href="/wiki/Panharmonicon" title="Panharmonicon">Panharmonicon</a>.
