This Beethoven also transcribed for orchestra as <i><a href="/wiki/Wellington%27s_Victory" title="Wellington's Victory">Wellington's Victory</a></i> (Op. 91, also known as the <i>Battle Symphony</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[n 9]</a></sup> It was first performed on 8 December, along with his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._7_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 7 (Beethoven)">Seventh Symphony</a>, Op. 92, at a charity concert for victims of the war, a concert whose success led to its repeat on 12 December. The orchestra included several leading and rising musicians who happened to be in Vienna at the time, including <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Meyerbeer" title="Giacomo Meyerbeer">Giacomo Meyerbeer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Dragonetti" title="Domenico Dragonetti">Domenico Dragonetti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a559–565_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a559–565-119">[110]</a></sup> The work received repeat performances at concerts staged by Beethoven in January and February 1814.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a575–576_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a575–576-120">[111]</a></sup> These concerts brought Beethoven more profit than any others in his career, and enabled him to buy the bank shares that were eventually to be the most valuable assets in his estate at his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScherer2004112_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScherer2004112-121">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's renewed popularity led to demands for a revival of <i>Fidelio</i>, which, in its third revised version, was also well received at its July opening in Vienna, and was frequently staged there during the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998288,_348_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998288,_348-122">[113]</a></sup> Beethoven's publishers, <a href="/wiki/Artaria" title="Artaria">Artaria</a>, commissioned the 20-year old Moscheles to prepare a piano score of the opera, which he inscribed "Finished, with God's help!" – to which Beethoven added "O Man, help thyself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[n 10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway2012129_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway2012129-124">[114]</a></sup> That summer Beethoven composed a piano sonata for the first time in five years, his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._27_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 27 (Beethoven)">Sonata in E minor, Opus 90</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998292_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998292-125">[115]</a></sup> He was also one of many composers who produced music in a patriotic vein to entertain the many heads of state and diplomats who came to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> that began in November 1814, with the cantata <i><a href="/wiki/Der_glorreiche_Augenblick" title="Der glorreiche Augenblick">Der glorreiche Augenblick</a> (The Glorious Moment)</i> (Op. 136) and similar choral works which, in the words of Maynard Solomon "broadened Beethoven's popularity, [but] did little to enhance his reputation as a serious composer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998287_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998287-126">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April and May 1814, playing in his <i>Archduke</i> Trio, Beethoven made his last public appearances as a soloist. The composer <a href="/wiki/Louis_Spohr" title="Louis Spohr">Louis Spohr</a> noted: "the piano was badly out of tune, which Beethoven minded little, since he did not hear it ... there was scarcely anything left of the virtuosity of the artist ... I was deeply saddened."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a577–578_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a577–578-127">[117]</a></sup> From 1814 onwards Beethoven used for conversation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ear-trumpet" title="Ear-trumpet">ear-trumpets</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nepomuk_Maelzel" title="Johann Nepomuk Maelzel">Johann Nepomuk Maelzel</a> (a number of these are on display at the Beethoven-Haus in Bonn).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaly1994266–267_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaly1994266–267-128">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>His 1815 compositions include an expressive second setting of the poem <i>An die Hoffnung</i> (Op. 94) in 1815. Compared to its first setting in 1805 (a gift for Josephine Brunsvik), it was "far more dramatic ... The entire spirit is that of an operatic scena."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278-129">[119]</a></sup> But his energy seemed to be dropping: apart from these works, he wrote the two <a href="/wiki/Cello_Sonatas_Nos._4_and_5_(Beethoven)" title="Cello Sonatas Nos. 4 and 5 (Beethoven)">cello sonatas Op. 102 nos. 1 and 2</a>, and a few minor pieces, and began but abandoned a sixth piano concerto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199624–25_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199624–25-130">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pause">Pause</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="440" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/170px-Beethoven_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/255px-Beethoven_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/340px-Beethoven_7.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1818 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=August_Kl%C3%B6ber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="August Klöber (page does not exist)">August Klöber</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_von_Kloeber" title="de:August von Kloeber">de</a>]</span></div></div></div><p>Between 1815 and 1819 Beethoven's output dropped again to a level unique in his mature life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998296_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998296-131">[121]</a></sup> He attributed part of this to a lengthy illness (he called it an inflammatory fever) that he had for more than a year, starting in October 1816.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008254_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008254-132">[122]</a></sup> His biographer Maynard Solomon suggests it is also doubtless a consequence of the ongoing legal problems concerning his nephew Karl,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998297_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998297-133">[123]</a></sup> and of Beethoven finding himself increasingly at odds with current musical trends. Unsympathetic to developments in German romanticism that featured the supernatural (as in operas by Spohr, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Marschner" title="Heinrich Marschner">Heinrich Marschner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Maria_von_Weber" title="Carl Maria von Weber">Carl Maria von Weber</a>), he also "resisted the impending Romantic fragmentation of the ... cyclic forms of the Classical era into small forms and lyric mood pieces" and turned towards study of Bach, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Frederic_Handel" title="George Frederic Handel">Handel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998295_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998295-134">[124]</a></sup> An old connection was renewed in 1817 when Maelzel sought, and obtained, Beethoven's endorsement for his newly developed <a href="/wiki/Metronome" title="Metronome">metronome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b684–686_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b684–686-135">[125]</a></sup> During these years the few major works he completed include the 1818 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hammerklavier_Sonata" title="Hammerklavier Sonata"><i>Hammerklavier</i> Sonata</a> (Sonata No. 29 in B flat major, Op. 106) and his settings of poems by <a href="/wiki/Alois_Jeitteles" title="Alois Jeitteles">Alois Jeitteles</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/An_die_ferne_Geliebte" title="An die ferne Geliebte">An die ferne Geliebte</a></i> Op. 98 (1816), which introduced the <a href="/wiki/Song_cycle" title="Song cycle">song cycle</a> into classical repertoire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014675–677_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014675–677-136">[126]</a></sup> In 1818 he began musical sketches that were eventually to form part of his final Ninth Symphony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998322_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998322-137">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>By early 1818 Beethoven's health had improved, and his nephew Karl, now aged 11, moved in with him in January (although within a year Karl's mother had won him back in the courts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199641_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199641-138">[128]</a></sup> By now Beethoven's hearing had again seriously deteriorated, necessitating Beethoven and his interlocutors writing in notebooks to carry out conversations. These 'conversation books' are a rich written resource for his life from this period onwards. They contain discussions about music, business, and personal life; they are also a valuable source for his contacts and for investigations into how he intended his music should be performed, and of his opinions of the art of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996164–167_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996164–167-139">[129]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[n 11]</a></sup> His household management had also improved somewhat with the help of <a href="/wiki/Nannette_Streicher" title="Nannette Streicher">Nannette Streicher</a>. A proprietor of the Stein piano workshop and a personal friend, Streicher had assisted in Beethoven's care during his illness; she continued to provide some support, and in her he finally found a skilled cook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008260_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008260-146">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[136]</a></sup> A testimonial to the esteem in which Beethoven was held in England was the presentation to him in this year by Thomas Broadwood, the proprietor of the company, of a <a href="/wiki/John_Broadwood_%26_Sons" title="John Broadwood &amp; Sons">Broadwood piano</a>, for which Beethoven expressed thanks. He was not well enough, however, to carry out a visit to London that year which had been proposed by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Philharmonic_Society" title="Royal Philharmonic Society">Philharmonic Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b696–698_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b696–698-148">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[n 12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurgence">Resurgence</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg/170px-Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg/255px-Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg/340px-Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Van_Beethoven_LCCN2003663902.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1819: portrait by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ferdinand_Schimon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ferdinand Schimon (page does not exist)">Ferdinand Schimon</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Schimon" title="de:Ferdinand Schimon">de</a>]</span></div></div></div><p>Despite the time occupied by his ongoing legal struggles over Karl, which involved continuing extensive correspondence and lobbying,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b790_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b790-151">[139]</a></sup> two events sparked off Beethoven's major composition projects in 1819. The first was the announcement of Archduke Rudolf's promotion to <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(Catholic_Church)" title="Cardinal (Catholic Church)">Cardinal</a>-<a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">Archbishop</a> as Archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Olomouc" title="Olomouc">Olomouc</a> (now in the Czech Republic), which triggered the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> Op. 123, intended to be ready for his installation in Olomouc in March 1820. The other was the invitation by the publisher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonio_Diabelli" title="Antonio Diabelli">Antonio Diabelli</a> to fifty Viennese composers, including Beethoven, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a>, Czerny and the 8-year old <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>, to compose a variation each on a theme which he provided. Beethoven was spurred to outdo the competition and by mid-1819 had already completed 20 variations of what were to become the 33 <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i> op. 120. Neither of these works was to be completed for a few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_8_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_8-152">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199645_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199645-153">[141]</a></sup> A significant tribute of 1819, however, was Archduke Rudolf's set of forty piano variations on a theme written for him by Beethoven (WoO 200) and dedicated to the master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b741,_745_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b741,_745-154">[142]</a></sup> Beethoven's portrait by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ferdinand_Schimon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ferdinand Schimon (page does not exist)">Ferdinand Schimon</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Schimon" title="de:Ferdinand Schimon">de</a>]</span> of this year, which was one of the most familiar images of him for the next century, was described by Schindler as, despite its artistic weaknesses, "in the rendering of that particular look, the majestic forehead ... the firmly shut mouth and the chin shaped like a shell, ... truer to nature than any other picture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b742_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b742-155">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's determination over the following years to write the <i>Mass</i> for Rudolf was not motivated by any devout <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholicism</a>. Although he had been born a Catholic, the form of religion as practised at the court in Bonn where he grew up was, in the words of Maynard Solomon, "a compromise ideology that permitted a relatively peaceful coexistence between the Church and <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon199854_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon199854-156">[144]</a></sup> Beethoven's <i>Tagebuch</i> (a diary he kept on an occasional basis between 1812 and 1818) shows his interest in a variety of religious philosophies, including those of India, Egypt and the Orient and the writings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rig-Veda" title="Rig-Veda">Rig-Veda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996146–147_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996146–147-157">[145]</a></sup> In a letter to Rudolf of July 1821, Beethoven shows his belief in a personal God: "God ... sees into my innermost heart and knows that as a man I perform most conscientiously and on all occasions the duties which Humanity, God, and Nature enjoin upon me." On one of the sketches for the <i>Missa solemnis</i> he wrote "Plea for inner and outer peace".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998342_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998342-158">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's status was confirmed by the series of <i>Concerts sprituels</i> given in Vienna by the choirmaster Franz Xaver Gebauer in the 1819/1820 and 1820/1821 seasons, during which all eight of his symphonies to date, plus the oratorio <i>Christus</i> and the Mass in C, were performed. Beethoven was typically underwhelmed: when in an April 1820 conversation book a friend mentioned Gebauer, Beethoven wrote in reply "Geh! Bauer" (Begone, peasant!)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b770–771(editor's_translation)_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b770–771(editor's_translation)-159">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_-_Piano_sonata_in_C_minor_(opus_111),_movement_1.ogg" title="File:Beethoven - Piano sonata in C minor (opus 111), movement 1.ogg">Piano Sonata No. 32 in C minor, Op. 111 (1st movement)</a></div>

<div class="description">written between 1821 and 1822, during Beethoven's late period</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>It was in 1819 that Beethoven was first approached by the publisher <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Schlesinger" title="Maurice Schlesinger">Moritz Schlesinger</a> who won the suspicious composer round, whilst visiting him at <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6dling" title="Mödling">Mödling</a>, by procuring for him a plate of roast veal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b734–735_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b734–735-160">[148]</a></sup> One consequence of this was that Schlesinger was to secure Beethoven's three last piano sonatas and his final quartets; part of the attraction to Beethoven was that Schlesinger had publishing facilities in Germany and France, and connections in England, which could overcome problems of copyright piracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway2012185–187_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway2012185–187-161">[149]</a></sup> The first of the three sonatas, for which Beethoven contracted with Schlesinger in 1820 at 30 <a href="/wiki/Ducat#Spread_of_the_ducat" title="Ducat">ducats</a> per sonata (further delaying completion of the Mass), was sent to the publisher at the end of that year (the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._30_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 30 (Beethoven)">Sonata in E major, Op. 109</a>, dedicated to Maximiliane, Antonie Brentano's daughter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199627–28_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199627–28-162">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The start of 1821 saw Beethoven once again in poor health, having <a href="/wiki/Rheumatism" title="Rheumatism">rheumatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a>. Despite this he continued work on the remaining piano sonatas he had promised to Schlesinger (the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._31_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 31 (Beethoven)">Sonata in A flat major Op. 110</a> was published in December), and on the Mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b776–777,_781–782_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b776–777,_781–782-163">[151]</a></sup> In early 1822 Beethoven sought a reconciliation with his brother Johann, whose marriage in 1812 had met with his disapproval, and Johann now became a regular visitor (as witnessed by the conversation books of the period) and began to assist him in his business affairs, including him lending him money against ownership of some of his compositions. He also sought some reconciliation with the mother of his nephew, including supporting her income, although this did not meet with the approval of the contrary Karl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998362–363_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998362–363-164">[152]</a></sup> Two commissions at the end of 1822 improved Beethoven's financial prospects. In November the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philharmonic_Society" title="Philharmonic Society">Philharmonic Society</a> of London offered a commission for a symphony, which he accepted with delight, as an appropriate home for the Ninth Symphony on which he was working.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b833–834_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b833–834-165">[153]</a></sup> Also in November Prince <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Borisovich_Galitzin" title="Nikolai Borisovich Galitzin">Nikolai Galitzin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> offered to pay Beethoven's asking price for three string quartets. Beethoven set the price at the high level of 50 ducats per quartet in a letter dictated to his nephew Karl, who was then living with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b815–816_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b815–816-166">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>During 1822, Anton Schindler, who in 1840 became one of Beethoven's earliest and most influential (but not always reliable) biographers, began to work as the composer's unpaid secretary. He was later to claim that he had been a member of Beethoven's circle since 1814, but there is no evidence for this. Cooper suggests that "Beethoven greatly appreciated his assistance, but did not think much of him as a man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199652,_309–310_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199652,_309–310-167">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1823.E2.80.931827:_The_final_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1823–1827:_The_final_years">1823–1827: The final years</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1226" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg/150px-Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg/225px-Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg/300px-Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Waldmuller_1823.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven in 1823 by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller" title="Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller">Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller</a></div></div></div>
<p>The year 1823 saw the completion of three notable works, all of which had occupied Beethoven for some years, namely the <i>Missa solemnis</i>, the Ninth Symphony and the <i>Diabelli Variations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b879_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b879-168">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven at last presented the manuscript of the completed <i>Missa</i> to Rudolph on 19 March (more than a year after the archduke's enthronement as archbishop). He was not however in a hurry to get it published or performed as he had formed a notion that he could profitably sell manuscripts of the work to various courts in Germany and Europe at 50 ducats each. One of the few who took up this offer was <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII" title="Louis XVIII">Louis XVIII</a> of France, who also sent Beethoven a heavy gold medallion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b812–829_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b812–829-169">[157]</a></sup> The Symphony and the variations took up most of the rest of Beethoven's working year. Diabelli hoped to publish both works, but the potential prize of the Mass excited many other publishers to lobby Beethoven for it, including Schlesinger and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Peters" title="Carl Friedrich Peters">Carl Friedrich Peters</a>. (In the end, it was obtained by <a href="/wiki/Schott_Music" title="Schott Music">Schotts</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway2012186_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway2012186-170">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Beethoven had become critical of the Viennese reception of his works. He told the visiting <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Rochlitz" title="Johann Friedrich Rochlitz">Johann Friedrich Rochlitz</a> in 1822:</p><blockquote><p>You will hear nothing of me here ... <i>Fidelio</i>? They cannot give it, nor do they want to listen to it. The symphonies? They have no time for them. My concertos? Everyone grinds out only the stuff he himself has made. The solo pieces? They went out of fashion long ago, and here fashion is everything. At the most, <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Schuppanzigh" title="Ignaz Schuppanzigh">Schuppanzigh</a> occasionally digs up a quartet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b801_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b801-171">[159]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> He, therefore, enquired about premiering the <i>Missa</i> and the Ninth Symphony in Berlin. When his Viennese admirers learnt of this, they pleaded with him to arrange local performances. Beethoven was won over, and the symphony was first performed, along with sections of the <i>Missa solemnis</i>, on 7 May 1824, to great acclaim at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%A4rntnertortheater" title="Kärntnertortheater">Kärntnertortheater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_9_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_9-172">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[n 13]</a></sup> Beethoven stood by the conductor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Umlauf" title="Michael Umlauf">Michael Umlauf</a> during the concert beating time (although Umlauf had warned the singers and orchestra to ignore him), and because of his deafness was not even aware of the applause which followed until he was turned to witness it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998351_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998351-175">[162]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_musikalische_Zeitung" title="Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung">Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</a></i> gushed, "inexhaustible genius had shown us a new world", and Carl Czerny wrote that the Symphony "breathes such a fresh, lively, indeed youthful spirit ... so much power, innovation, and beauty as ever [came] from the head of this original man, although he certainly sometimes led the old wigs to shake their heads". The concert did not net Beethoven much money, as the expenses of mounting it were very high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008317_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008317-176">[163]</a></sup> A second concert on 24 May, in which the producer guaranteed him a minimum fee, was poorly attended; nephew Karl noted that "many people [had] already gone into the country". It was Beethoven's last public concert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318-177">[164]</a></sup> Beethoven accused Schindler of either cheating him or mismanaging the ticket receipts; this led to the replacement of Schindler as Beethoven's secretary by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Holz_(violinist)" title="Karl Holz (violinist)">Karl Holz</a>, the second violinist in the <a href="/wiki/Schuppanzigh_Quartet" title="Schuppanzigh Quartet">Schuppanzigh Quartet</a>, although by 1826 Beethoven and Schindler were reconciled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996310_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996310-178">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven then turned to writing the string quartets for Galitzin, despite failing health. The first of these, the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._12_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 12 (Beethoven)">quartet in E♭ major, Op. 127</a> was premiered by the Schuppanzigh Quartet in March 1825. While writing the next, the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._15_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 15 (Beethoven)">quartet in A minor, Op. 132</a>, in April 1825, he was struck by a sudden illness. Recuperating in <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a>, he included in the quartet its slow movement to which he gave the title "Holy song of thanks (Heiliger Dankgesang) to the Divinity, from a convalescent, in the <a href="/wiki/Lydian_mode" title="Lydian mode">Lydian mode</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup> The next quartet to be completed was the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._13_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 13 (Beethoven)">Thirteenth, op. 130</a>, in B♭ major. In six movements, the last, contrapuntal movement proved to be very difficult for both the performers and the audience at its premiere in March 1826 (again by the Schuppanzigh Quartet). Beethoven was persuaded by the publisher <a href="/wiki/Artaria" title="Artaria">Artaria</a>, for an additional fee, to write a new finale, and to issue the last movement as a separate work (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grosse_Fugue" title="Grosse Fugue">Grosse Fugue</a>, Op. 133).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b974–975_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b974–975-179">[166]</a></sup> Beethoven's favourite was the last of this series, the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">quartet in C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">♯</span></span> minor Op. 131</a>, which he rated as his most perfect single work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2010213_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2010213-180">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's relations with his nephew Karl had continued to be stormy; Beethoven's letters to him were demanding and reproachful. In August, Karl, who had been seeing his mother again against Beethoven's wishes, attempted suicide by shooting himself in the head. He survived and after discharge from hospital went to recuperate in the village of <a href="/wiki/Gneixendorf" title="Gneixendorf">Gneixendorf</a> with Beethoven and his uncle Johann. Whilst in Gneixendorf, Beethoven completed a further quartet (<a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._16_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 16 (Beethoven)">Op. 135 in F major</a>) which he sent to Schlesinger. Under the introductory slow chords in the last movement, Beethoven wrote in the manuscript "Muss es sein?" (Must it be?); the response, over the faster main theme of the movement, is "Es muss sein!" (It must be!). The whole movement is headed <i>Der schwer gefasste Entschluss</i> (The difficult decision).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b977_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b977-181">[168]</a></sup> Following this in November Beethoven completed his final composition, the replacement finale for the op. 130 quartet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup> Beethoven at this time was already ill and depressed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup> he began to quarrel with Johann, insisting that Johann made Karl his heir, in preference to Johann's wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1014_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1014-182">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Death of Ludwig van Beethoven">Death of Ludwig van Beethoven</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1443" data-file-width="1923" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg/220px-Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg/330px-Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg/440px-Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_on_his_deathbed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven on his deathbed; sketch by <a href="/wiki/Josef_Danhauser" title="Josef Danhauser">Josef Danhauser</a></div></div></div><p>On his return journey to Vienna from Gneixendorf in December 1826, illness struck Beethoven again. He was attended until his death by Dr. Andreas Wawruch, who throughout December noticed symptoms including fever, <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dropsy" title="Dropsy">dropsy</a>, with swollen limbs, coughing and breathing difficulties. Several operations were carried out to tap off the excess fluid from Beethoven's abdomen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1017–1024_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1017–1024-183">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Karl stayed by Beethoven's bedside during December, but left after the beginning of January to join the army at <a href="/wiki/Jihlava" title="Jihlava">Iglau</a> and did not see his uncle again, although he wrote to him shortly afterwards: "My dear father ... I am living in contentment and regret only that I am separated from you." Immediately following Karl's departure, Beethoven wrote a will making his nephew his sole heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998377_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998377-184">[171]</a></sup> Later in January, Beethoven was attended by Dr. Malfatti, whose treatment (recognizing the seriousness of his patient's condition) was largely centred on alcohol. As the news spread of the severity of Beethoven's condition, many old friends came to visit, including Diabelli, Schuppanzigh, Lichnowsky, Schindler, the composer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nepomuk_Hummel" title="Johann Nepomuk Hummel">Johann Nepomuk Hummel</a> and his pupil <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Hiller" title="Ferdinand Hiller">Ferdinand Hiller</a>. Many tributes and gifts were also sent, including £100 from the Philharmonic Society in London and a case of expensive wine from Schotts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998378–379_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998378–379-185">[172]</a></sup> During this period, Beethoven was almost completely bedridden despite occasional efforts to rouse himself. On 24 March, he said to Schindler and the others present "Plaudite, amici, comoedia finita est" ("Applaud, friends, the comedy is over"). Later that day, when the wine from Schott arrived, he whispered, "Pity – too late."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998380–381_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998380–381-186">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Funerals.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1858" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Beethoven_Funerals.jpg/220px-Beethoven_Funerals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Beethoven_Funerals.jpg/330px-Beethoven_Funerals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Beethoven_Funerals.jpg/440px-Beethoven_Funerals.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Funerals.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's funeral procession: watercolour by F. X. Stoeber</div></div></div><p>Beethoven died on 26 March 1827 at the age of 56; only his friend <a href="/wiki/Anselm_H%C3%BCttenbrenner" title="Anselm Hüttenbrenner">Anselm Hüttenbrenner</a> and a "Frau van Beethoven" (possibly his old enemy Johanna van Beethoven) were present. According to Hüttenbrenner, at about 5 pm there was a flash of lightning and a clap of thunder: "Beethoven opened his eyes, lifted his right hand and looked up for several seconds with his fist clenched ... not another breath, not a heartbeat more."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1050–1051_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1050–1051-187">[174]</a></sup> Many visitors came to the death-bed; some locks of the dead man's hair were retained by Hüttenbrenner and Hiller, amongst others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998381–382_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998381–382-188">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway201244_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway201244-189">[176]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a> revealed Beethoven had significant <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> damage, which may have been due to his heavy alcohol consumption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349-190">[177]</a></sup> and also considerable dilation of the auditory and other related nerves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaccentiSmildeSaris2011_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaccentiSmildeSaris2011-191">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaccentiSmildeSaris2012_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaccentiSmildeSaris2012-192">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[n 14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's funeral procession in Vienna on 29 March 1827 was attended by an estimated 10,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaruskin2010687_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaruskin2010687-198">[184]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a> and the violinist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Mayseder" title="Joseph Mayseder">Joseph Mayseder</a> were among the torchbearers. A funeral oration by the poet <a href="/wiki/Franz_Grillparzer" title="Franz Grillparzer">Franz Grillparzer</a> was read by the actor <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Ansch%C3%BCtz" title="Heinrich Anschütz">Heinrich Anschütz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[185]</a></sup> Beethoven was buried in the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4hring" title="Währing">Währing</a> cemetery, north-west of Vienna, after a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Requiem_mass" title="Requiem mass">requiem mass</a> at the church of the Holy Trinity (Dreifaltigkeitskirche) in Alserstrasse. Beethoven's remains were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exhume" title="Exhume">exhumed</a> for study in 1863, and moved in 1888 to Vienna's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zentralfriedhof" title="Zentralfriedhof">Zentralfriedhof</a> where they were reinterred in a grave adjacent to that of Schubert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318,_349-190">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1053–1056_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967b1053–1056-200">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_musical_style" title="Beethoven's musical style">Beethoven's musical style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_compositional_method" title="Beethoven's compositional method">Beethoven's compositional method</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_C_minor" title="Beethoven and C minor">Beethoven and C minor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven">List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_three_periods">The three periods</span></h3>
<p>The historian William Drabkin notes that as early as 1818 a writer had proposed a three-period division of Beethoven's works and that such a division (albeit often adopting different dates or works to denote changes in period) eventually became a convention adopted by all of Beethoven's biographers, starting with Schindler, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Joseph_F%C3%A9tis" title="François-Joseph Fétis">F.-J. Fétis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Lenz" title="Wilhelm von Lenz">Wilhelm von Lenz</a>. Later writers sought to identify sub-periods within this generally accepted structure. Its drawbacks include that it generally omits a fourth period, that is, the early years in Bonn, whose works are less often considered; and that it ignores the differential development of Beethoven's composing styles over the years for different categories of work. The piano sonatas, for example, were written throughout Beethoven's life in a progression that can be interpreted as continuous development; the symphonies do not all demonstrate linear progress; of all of the types of composition, perhaps the quartets, which seem to group themselves in three periods (Op. 18 in 1801–1802, Opp. 59, 74 and 95 in 1806–1814, and the quartets, today known as 'late', from 1824 onwards) fit this categorization most neatly. Drabkin concludes that "now that we have lived with them so long ... as long as there are programme notes, essays written to accompany recordings, and all-Beethoven recitals, it is hard to imagine us ever giving up the notion of discrete stylistic periods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996198–200_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996198–200-201">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_11_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_11-202">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bonn_1782.E2.80.931792"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonn_1782–1792">Bonn 1782–1792</span></h3>
<p>Some forty compositions, including ten very early works written by Beethoven up to 1785, survive from the years that Beethoven lived in Bonn. It has been suggested that Beethoven largely abandoned composition between 1785 and 1790, possibly as a result of negative critical reaction to his first published works. A 1784 review in <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nikolaus_Forkel" title="Johann Nikolaus Forkel">Johann Nikolaus Forkel</a>'s influential <i>Musikalischer Almanack</i> compared Beethoven's efforts to those of rank beginners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1972169_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1972169-203">[189]</a></sup> The three early piano quartets of 1785 (WoO 36), closely modelled on violin sonatas of Mozart, show his dependency on the music of the period. Beethoven himself was not to give any of the Bonn works an opus number, save for those which he reworked for use later in his career, for example, some of the songs in his Op. 52 collection (1805) and the <a href="/wiki/Octet_(Beethoven)" title="Octet (Beethoven)">Wind Octet</a> reworked in Vienna in 1793 to become his <a href="/wiki/String_Quintet,_Op._4_(Beethoven)" title="String Quintet, Op. 4 (Beethoven)">String Quintet, Op. 4</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham200112_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham200112-204">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996227,_230_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996227,_230-205">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Rosen" title="Charles Rosen">Charles Rosen</a> points out that Bonn was something of a backwater compared to Vienna; Beethoven was unlikely to be acquainted with the mature works of Haydn or Mozart, and Rosen opines that his early style was closer to that of Hummel or <a href="/wiki/Muzio_Clementi" title="Muzio Clementi">Muzio Clementi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERosen1972379–380_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosen1972379–380-206">[192]</a></sup> Kernan suggests that at this stage Beethoven was not especially notable for his works in <a href="/wiki/Sonata" title="Sonata">sonata</a> style, but more for his vocal music; his move to Vienna in 1792 set him on the path to develop the music in the genres he became known for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham200112_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham200112-204">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_period">The first period</span></h3>
<p>The conventional first period begins after Beethoven's arrival in Vienna in 1792. In the first few years he seems to have composed less than he did at Bonn, and his Piano Trios, op.1 were not published until 1795. From this point onward, he had mastered the 'Viennese style' (best known today from <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Haydn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a>) and was making the style his own. His works from 1795 to 1800 are larger in scale than was the norm (writing sonatas in four movements, not three, for instance); typically he uses a <a href="/wiki/Scherzo" title="Scherzo">scherzo</a> rather than a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minuet_and_trio" title="Minuet and trio">minuet and trio</a>; and his music often includes dramatic, even sometimes over-the-top, uses of extreme dynamics and tempi and chromatic harmony. It was this that led Haydn to believe the third trio of Op.1 was too difficult for an audience to appreciate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_13_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§_13-207">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also explored new directions and gradually expanded the scope and ambition of his work. Some important pieces from the early period are the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">first</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">second</a> symphonies, the set of six string quartets <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">Opus 18</a>, the first two piano concertos, and the first dozen or so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_piano_sonatas" title="Beethoven's piano sonatas">piano sonatas</a>, including the famous <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 8 (Beethoven)">Pathétique</a></i> sonata, Op. 13.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_middle_period">The middle period</span></h3>
<p>His middle period began shortly after the personal crisis brought on by his recognition of encroaching deafness. It includes large-scale works that express heroism and struggle. Middle-period works include six symphonies (Nos. 3–8), the last two piano concertos, the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Triple Concerto (Beethoven)">Triple Concerto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">violin concerto</a>, five string quartets (Nos. 7–11), several piano sonatas (including the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._23_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 23 (Beethoven)">Appassionata</a></i> sonatas), the <i><a href="/wiki/Violin_Sonata_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Sonata No. 9 (Beethoven)">Kreutzer</a></i> violin sonata and his only <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.
</p><p>This period is sometimes associated with a <i>heroic</i> manner of composing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1990124_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1990124-208">[194]</a></sup> but the use of the term "heroic" has become increasingly controversial in Beethoven scholarship. The term is more frequently used as an alternative name for the middle period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinberg2006_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steinberg2006-209">[195]</a></sup> The appropriateness of the term heroic to describe the whole middle period has been questioned as well: while some works, like the Third and Fifth Symphonies, are easy to describe as heroic, many others, like his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Symphony No. 6, <i>Pastoral</i></a> or his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._24_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 24 (Beethoven)">Piano Sonata No. 24</a>, are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurnhamSteinberg2000_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-BurnhamSteinberg2000-210">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_late_period">The late period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/170px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/255px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG/340px-Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zentralfriedhof_Vienna_-_Beethoven.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beethoven's grave at Vienna <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zentralfriedhof" title="Zentralfriedhof">Zentralfriedhof</a></div></div></div><p>Beethoven's late period began in the decade 1810-1819.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrendel199160–61_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel199160–61-211">[197]</a></sup> He began a renewed study of older music, including works by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a>, whom Beethoven considered "the greatest composer who ever lived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1970126–127_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1970126–127-212">[198]</a></sup> Beethoven's late works incorporated <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque</a>-era devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrendel199162_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel199162-213">[199]</a></sup> For example, the overture <i><a href="/wiki/The_Consecration_of_the_House_(overture)" title="The Consecration of the House (overture)">The Consecration of the House</a></i> (1822) included a fugue influenced by Handel's music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1970347–348_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1970347–348-214">[200]</a></sup> A new style emerged, as he returned to the keyboard to compose his first piano sonatas in almost a decade; the works of the late period include the last five piano sonatas and the <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i>, the last two sonatas for cello and piano, the <a href="/wiki/Late_string_quartets_(Beethoven)" title="Late string quartets (Beethoven)">late string quartets</a> (including the massive <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gro%C3%9Fe_Fuge" title="Große Fuge">Große Fuge</a></i>), and two works for very large forces: the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper197010_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper197010-215">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrendel199160–63_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel199160–63-216">[202]</a></sup> Works from this period are characterised by their intellectual depth, their formal innovations, and their intense, highly personal expression. The <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 14 (Beethoven)">String Quartet, Op. 131</a> has seven linked movements, and the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a> adds choral forces to the orchestra in the last movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001-217">[203]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span id="Beethoven.27s_pianos"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Beethoven's_pianos">Beethoven's pianos</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven's earlier preferred pianos included those of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Andreas_Stein" title="Johann Andreas Stein">Johann Andreas Stein</a>; he may have been given a Stein piano by Count Waldstein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman1970486_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman1970486-218">[204]</a></sup> From 1786 onwards there is evidence of Beethoven's cooperation with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Andreas_Streicher" title="Johann Andreas Streicher">Johann Andreas Streicher</a>, who had married Stein's daughter <a href="/wiki/Nannette_Streicher" title="Nannette Streicher">Nannette</a>. Streicher left Stein's business to set up his own firm in 1803, and Beethoven continued to admire his products, writing to him in 1817 of his "special preference" for his pianos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman1970486_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman1970486-218">[204]</a></sup> Amongst the other pianos Beethoven possessed was an <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9bastien_%C3%89rard" title="Sébastien Érard">Érard</a> piano given to him by the manufacturer in 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002523_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002523-219">[205]</a></sup> The Érard piano, with its exceptional resonance, may have influenced Beethoven's piano style – shortly after receiving it he began writing his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein Sonata</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002530-531_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002530-531-220">[206]</a></sup> – but despite initial enthusiasm he seems to have abandoned it before 1810, when he wrote that it was "simply not of any use any more"; in 1824 he gave it to his brother Johann.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002530-531_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002530-531-220">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[n 15]</a></sup> In 1818 Beethoven received, also as gift, a grand piano by <a href="/wiki/John_Broadwood_%26_Sons" title="John Broadwood &amp; Sons">John Broadwood &amp; Sons</a>. Although Beethoven was proud to receive it, he seems to have been dissatisfied by its tone (a dissatisfaction which was perhaps also a consequence of his increasing deafness), and sought to get it remodelled to make it louder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman1970489-90_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman1970489-90-223">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[n 16]</a></sup> In 1825 Beethoven commissioned a piano from <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Graf" title="Conrad Graf">Conrad Graf</a>, which was equipped with quadruple strings and a special resonator to make it audible to him, but which failed in this task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman1970491_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman1970491-226">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[n 17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_in_film" title="Beethoven in film">Beethoven in film</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3496" data-file-width="2464" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/170px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/255px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg/340px-Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_bust_statue_by_Hagen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Beethoven by <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Hagen" title="Hugo Hagen">Hugo Hagen</a>, 1892, <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>, Washington, D.C.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums">Museums</span></h3>
<p>There is a museum—the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a>, the place of his birth—in central Bonn. The same city has hosted a musical festival, the <a href="/wiki/Beethovenfest" title="Beethovenfest">Beethovenfest</a>, since 1845. The festival was initially irregular but has been organised annually since 2007.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ira_F._Brilliant_Center_for_Beethoven_Studies" title="Ira F. Brilliant Center for Beethoven Studies">Ira F. Brilliant Center for Beethoven Studies</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Dr._Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Library" title="Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. Library">Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. Library</a>, in the campus of <a href="/wiki/San_Jose_State_University" title="San Jose State University">San Jose State University</a>, California, serves as a museum, research center, and host of lectures and performances devoted solely to Beethoven's life and works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sculptures">Sculptures</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sculptures_of_Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="List of sculptures of Ludwig van Beethoven">List of sculptures of Ludwig van Beethoven</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven_Monument_(Bonn)" title="Beethoven Monument (Bonn)">Beethoven Monument</a> in Bonn was unveiled in August 1845, in honour of the 75th anniversary of his birth. It was the first statue of a composer created in Germany, and the music festival that accompanied the unveiling was the impetus for the very hasty construction of the original <a href="/wiki/Beethovenhalle" title="Beethovenhalle">Beethovenhalle</a> in Bonn (it was designed and built within less than a month, on the urging of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>). Vienna honoured Beethoven with a statue in 1880.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComini2008316_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComini2008316-230">[213]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space">Space</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_(crater)" title="Beethoven (crater)">third-largest crater</a> on Mercury is named in his honour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[214]</a></sup> as is the main-belt asteroid <a href="/wiki/1815_Beethoven" title="1815 Beethoven">1815 Beethoven</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beethoven's music features twice on the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_Golden_Record" title="Voyager Golden Record">Voyager Golden Record</a>, a phonograph record containing a broad sample of the images, common sounds, languages, and music of Earth, sent into outer space with the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> probes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_Record_Music_List_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_Record_Music_List-233">[216]</a></sup>
</p>

href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">LUUD</span>-vig van <span style="font-size:90%">BAY</span>-toh-vən</i></a>; <small>German: </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈluːtvɪç fan ˈbeːtˌhoːfn̩]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" title="De-Ludwig van Beethoven.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The prefix <i>van</i> to the surname Beethoven reflects the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_in_Belgium" title="Dutch in Belgium">Flemish</a> origins of the family name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199636_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199636-2">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most of Beethoven's early works and those to which he did not give an <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">opus number</a> were listed by Georg Kinsky and Hans Halm as "<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a>", works without opus number. Kinsky and Halm also listed 18 doubtful works in their appendix ("WoO Anhang"). In addition, some minor works not listed with opus numbers or in the WoO list have <a href="/wiki/Catalogues_of_Beethoven_compositions#Hess_catalogue" title="Catalogues of Beethoven compositions">Hess catalogue</a> numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996210_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996210-15">[13]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is uncertain whether this was the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> (Op. 15) or <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a> (Op. 19 which was in fact written earlier than Op. 15). Documentary evidence is lacking, and both concertos were still in manuscript (neither was completed or published for several years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-45">[42]</a></sup> Some authorities favour Op. 15,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996221_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996221-46">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2014174-5_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2014174-5-47">[44]</a></sup> but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_Music_Online" title="Oxford Music Online">Oxford Music Online</a> suggests it was probably Op. 19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§3_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§3-48">[45]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The cause of Beethoven's deafness has also variously been attributed to, amongst other possibilities, <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a> from Beethoven's preferred wines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevens20132854–2858_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevens20132854–2858-66">[62]</a></sup> Another possibility is that it was caused by complications from a case of <a href="/wiki/Murine_typhus" title="Murine typhus">murine typhus</a> from 1796.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Solomon sets out his case in detail in his biography of Beethoven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998223–231_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998223–231-98">[93]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The manuscript (now lost) was found in Therese Malfatti's papers after her death by Beethoven's early biographer <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Nohl" title="Ludwig Nohl">Ludwig Nohl</a>. It has been suggested that Nohl misread the title, which may have been <i>Für Therese</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a502_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1967a502-104">[98]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Their ruling stated: "It ... appears from the statement of Ludwig van Beethoven  ... is unable to prove nobility: hence the matter of guardianship is transferred to the Magistrate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1998303-113">[106]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The work is not a true symphony, but a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programme_music" title="Programme music">programmatic</a> piece including French and British soldiers' songs, a battle scene with artillery effects and a <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugal</a> treatment of "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_King" title="God Save the King">God Save the King</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1996220_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1996220-117">[109]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>Fine</i> mit Gottes Hülfe" – "O, Mensch, hilf dir selber."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was suggested by Beethoven's biographer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wheelock_Thayer" title="Alexander Wheelock Thayer">Alexander Wheelock Thayer</a> that, of 400 conversation books, 264 were destroyed (and others were altered) after his death by his secretary Schindler, who wished only an idealised biography to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClive2001239_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClive2001239-140">[130]</a></sup> The music historian <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Albrecht" title="Theodore Albrecht">Theodore Albrecht</a> has, however, demonstrated that Thayer's allegations were over the top. "[It is now] abundantly clear that Schindler never possessed as many as c. 400 conversation books, and that he never destroyed roughly five-eighths of that number."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbrecht2009181_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbrecht2009181-141">[131]</a></sup> Schindler did however insert a number of fraudulent entries that bolstered his own profile and his prejudices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199652_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199652-142">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl1994103_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl1994103-143">[133]</a></sup> Presently 136 books covering the period 1819–1827 are preserved at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staatsbibliothek_Berlin" title="Staatsbibliothek Berlin">Staatsbibliothek Berlin</a>, with another two at the Beethoven-Haus in Bonn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammelmann1965187_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammelmann1965187-144">[134]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Broadwood piano is now in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_National_Museum" title="Hungarian National Museum">Hungarian National Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper199643_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper199643-149">[138]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first full performance of the <i>Missa solemnis</i> had already been given in St. Petersburg by Galitzin, who had been a subscriber for the manuscript 'preview' that Beethoven had arranged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKermanTysonBurnham2001§10-173">[161]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-197">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is dispute about the actual cause of his death: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_cirrhosis" title="Alcoholic cirrhosis">alcoholic cirrhosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_hepatitis" title="Infectious hepatitis">infectious hepatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whipple%27s_disease" title="Whipple's disease">Whipple's disease</a> have all been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMai2006_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMai2006-193">[180]</a></sup> Surviving locks of his hair have been subjected to additional analysis, as have skull fragments removed during an 1863 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exhumation" title="Exhumation">exhumation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith20052–3_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith20052–3-194">[181]</a></sup> Some of these analyses have led to controversial assertions that he was accidentally <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisoned</a> by excessive doses of lead-based treatments administered under instruction from his doctor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEisinger2008_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEisinger2008-195">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorenz2007_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorenz2007-196">[183]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The piano is now in the Oberösterreichisches Landesmuseum in <a href="/wiki/Linz" title="Linz">Linz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002522_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkowroneck2002522-221">[207]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-225"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-225">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The piano is now in the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_National_Museum" title="Hungarian National Museum">Hungarian National Museum</a>, Budapest, to which it was donated by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>; it was restored to playing condition in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinston1993_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinston1993-224">[209]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-228"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-228">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Now in the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven-Haus</a>, Bonn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[211]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
